The center of the universal enveloping algebra of \(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C}\) is the one variable polynomial ring \(\mathbb{C}[\Omega]\), where \[\Omega := W^2 - \frac{X Y + Y X}{2} = W(W-1) - X Y = W(W+1) - Y X.\] The ring \(\mathop{\mathrm{Sym}}(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C})^G\) of \(G\)-invariant polynomials on \(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C}^*\) is generated by the polynomial \(\Lambda := W^2 - X Y\). The Harish–Chandra isomorphism \(\mathbb{C}[\Omega] \xrightarrow{\cong } \mathbb{C}[\Lambda]\) is given in this case by \(\Omega \mapsto \Lambda - 1/4\).

We identify \(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C}^*\) with \(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C}\) via the trace pairing \((x,\xi) \mapsto \langle x, \xi \rangle := \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(x \xi)\). We identify the real and imaginary duals \(\mathfrak{g}^*\) and \(i \mathfrak{g}^*\) of \(\mathfrak{g}\) with the subspaces of \(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C}^*\) taking real and imaginary values on \(\mathfrak{g}\), respectively. We abbreviate \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge := i \mathfrak{g}^*\); it identifies with the Pontryagin dual of \(\mathfrak{g}\) via the natural pairing \(\mathfrak{g} \times \mathfrak{g}^\wedge \ni (x,\xi) \mapsto e^{\langle x, \xi \rangle} \in \mathbb{C}^{(1)}\). We occasionally work with the coordinates and basis elements \[\mathfrak{g} \ni x = \begin{pmatrix} x_1/2 & x_2 \\ x_3 & -x_1/2 \end{pmatrix} = \sum_{j=1,2,3} x_j e_j,\] \[\mathfrak{g}^\wedge \ni \xi = i \begin{pmatrix} \xi_1 & \xi_3 \\ \xi_2 & -\xi_1 \end{pmatrix} = \sum_{j=1,2,3} \xi_j e_j^*,\] so that the natural pairing is given by \((x,\xi) \mapsto e^{i \sum x_j \xi_j}\). We note that the invariant polynomial \(\Lambda\) is given in this optic by \(\Lambda(\xi) = \det(\xi/i) = - \xi_1^2 - \xi_2 \xi_3\).

The following elements, defined for \(t \in \mathbb{R}_{\neq 0}\), will occur frequently in our analysis: \[\label{eqn:defn-xi-of-t}\tag{6.1} \xi(t) := i \begin{pmatrix} t & \\ & -t \end{pmatrix} \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge.\] We note that \(X(\xi(t)) = Y(\xi(t)) = t\), \(W(\xi(t)) = 0\) and \(\Lambda(\xi(t)) = -t^2\). We note also that the \(G\)-stabilizer of \(\xi(t)\) is the diagonal subgroup \(H\).

We fix norms \(|.|\) on all of the above spaces.

§6.2. Coadjoint orbits

A coadjoint orbit \(\mathcal{O}\) is a \(G\)-orbit on \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\); in particular, it is a smooth manifold. The origin \(\{0\}\) is a zero-dimensional coadjoint orbit. The other coadjoint orbits are two-dimensional and of the form \[\mathcal{O}(\lambda) := \{\text{$\xi \in\mathfrak{g}^\wedge - \{0\}$ with $\Lambda(\xi) = \lambda$}\}\] for some \(\lambda \in \mathbb{R}\). If \(\lambda = 0\), then \(\mathcal{O}(\lambda)\) is the regular subset of the nilcone; if \(\lambda > 0\), it is a two-sheeted hyperboloid; if \(\lambda < 0\), it is a one-sheeted hyperboloid. For \(t \neq 0\), the orbit of \(\xi(t)\) is \(\mathcal{O}(-t^2)\).

We equip any two-dimensional coadjoint orbit \(\mathcal{O}\) with its normalized symplectic measure \[C_c(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge) \ni a \mapsto \int_{\mathcal{O}} a := \int_{\xi \in \mathcal{O}} a(\xi) \, d \omega(\xi),\] defined by the \(2\)-form \(\omega\) on \(\mathcal{O}\) described as follows (see, e.g., (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 6) or (Kirillov 1999) for further details, and the calculations of §8.2 for some explicit formulas). For each \(\xi \in \mathcal{O}\), the tangent space \(T_\xi \mathcal{O}\) identifies with the space of vectors \(\{x \cdot \xi : x \in \mathfrak{g}\}\), where \(x \cdot \xi \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge\) is defined by differentiating the action of \(G\) on \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\). The component \(\omega_\xi\) of \(\omega\) at \(\xi\) is then given by \(\omega_{\xi}(x \cdot \xi, y \cdot \xi) := \langle \xi, [x,y] \rangle/2 \pi i\). For each \(a \in C_c(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge)\), the function \(\mathbb{R} \ni \lambda \mapsto \int_{\mathcal{O}(\lambda)} a\) is continuous and compactly-supported (see, e.g., (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 11.2)). The rescaling \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}\) is also a coadjoint orbit, and we have \(\int_{\xi \in \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}} a(\xi) \, d \omega (\xi ) = \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\int_{\xi \in \mathcal{O}} a(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\xi) \, d \omega (\xi )\). We record a simple estimate:

Lemma 17. Let \(\tau \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge\) with \(|\tau| \asymp 1\), and let \(0 < r \leqslant 1\). For any two-dimensional coadjoint orbit \(\mathcal{O}\), the symplectic volume of the subset \(\{\xi \in \mathcal{O} : |\xi - \tau| < r\}\) of \(\mathcal{O}\) is \(\operatorname{O}(r^2)\).

Proof. Each \(\tau \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge - \{0\}\) is regular, i.e., the differential of the invariant polynomial \(\Lambda\) is nonzero at \(\tau\). On a small neighborhood of each such \(\tau\), we may thus find local coordinates \((\tau_1,\tau_2,\Lambda)\) with respect to which the coadjoint orbits are the fibers of the projection onto the third coordinate, with the symplectic measures given by smooth multiples of Lebesgue measure in the first two coordinates. The required estimate follows for \(\tau\) in a small fixed neighborhood of each fixed element of \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge - \{0\}\), then in general by continuity and compactness. ◻

§6.3. Representations

Let \(\pi\) be an irreducible unitary representation of \(G\). Then \(\Omega\) acts on the smooth subspace of \(\pi\) by some real scalar \(\Omega_{\pi}\). We set \(\lambda_\pi := 1/4 + \Omega_\pi\), and refer to it as the infinitesimal character of \(\pi\). Up to isomorphism, we may classify \(\pi\) as follows:

  • The one-dimensional representations (the trivial representation \(\mathbb{C}\) and the sign representation \(\mathbb{C} (\mathop{\mathrm{sgn}}\circ \det)\)), for which \(\Omega_\pi = 0\) and \(\lambda_\pi = 1/4\).

  • The discrete series representations \(\pi(k)\) (\(k \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 1}\)), for which \(\Omega_\pi = k(k-1)\) and \(\lambda_\pi = (k-1/2)^2\). (We note that \(\pi(k)\) is often denoted \(\mathcal{D}_{2k}\).)

  • The unitary principal series representations \(\pi(t,\varepsilon)\), with

    1. \(t \in \mathbb{R}\) and \(\varepsilon\in \{\pm 1\}\) or

    2. \(t \in i(-1/2,1/2) - \{0\}\) and \(\varepsilon= 1\) (the “complementary series”),

    obtained by normalized parabolic induction of the character \(\mathop{\mathrm{diag}}(y,1) \mapsto \mathop{\mathrm{sgn}}(y)^\varepsilon|y|^{i t}\), for which \(\Omega_\pi = -1/4 - t^2\) and \(\lambda_\pi = - t^2\).

The only equivalences are that \(\pi(t,\varepsilon) \cong \pi(-t,\varepsilon)\). The tempered irreducibles are the \(\pi(k)\) and \(\pi(t,\varepsilon)\) with \(t \in \mathbb{R}\).

Suppose that \(\pi\) is not one-dimensional. We may then realize it as follows. If \(\pi = \pi(t,\varepsilon)\), set \(Q := \mathbb{Z}\); if \(\pi = \pi(k)\), set \(Q := \{q \in \mathbb{Z} : |q| \geqslant k\}\) and \(\varepsilon:= 1\). We regard \(L^2(Q)\) as a Hilbert space with respect to the counting measure, with basis elements given by the \(\delta\)-masses \(e_q\) at each \(q \in Q\). It contains the dense subspace \(C_c(Q)\) consisting of the finitely-supported elements. We verify readily that the following formulas define an infinitesimally unitary \((\mathfrak{g},K)\)-module structure on \(C_c(Q)\), corresponding to a representative for the isomorphism class of \(\pi\): \[X e_q = ( q(q+1) - \Omega_\pi)^{1/2} e_{q+1},\] \[Y e_{q+1} = (q(q+1) - \Omega_\pi)^{1/2} e_{q},\] \[W e_q = q e_q, \quad e^{i \theta W} e_q = e^{i \theta q} e_q, \quad \mathop{\mathrm{diag}}(-1,1) e_q = (-1)^{\varepsilon} e_{-q}.\]

§6.4. Kirillov formula

The character of an irreducible representation \(\pi\) of \(G\) is a generalized function \(\chi_\pi : G \rightarrow \mathbb{C}\) (see, e.g., (Knapp 1986, vol. 36, sec. X)). Fix a sufficiently small open neighborhood \(\mathcal{G}\) of the origin in \(\mathfrak{g}\). The normalized Jacobian of the exponential map is the function \(\mathop{\mathrm{jac}}: \mathcal{G} \rightarrow \mathbb{R}_{>0}\) for which

  • \(\mathop{\mathrm{jac}}(0) = 1\), and

  • if \(d g\) is any Haar measure on \(G\), then there is a unique Haar measure \(d x\) on \(\mathfrak{g}\) so that for \(g = \exp(x)\) with \(x \in \mathcal{G}\), we have \(d g = \mathop{\mathrm{jac}}(x) d x\). We say in this case that \(d g\) and \(d x\) are compatibly normalized.

Lemma 18. Let \(\pi\) be a tempered irreducible unitary representation of \(G\). Set \(\mathcal{O}_\pi := \mathcal{O}(\lambda_\pi)\). For \(x \in \mathcal{G}\), we have the identity of generalized functions \[\chi_\pi(\exp(x)) = \mathop{\mathrm{jac}}(x)^{-1/2} \int_{\xi \in \mathcal{O}_\pi} e^{\langle x, \xi \rangle} \, d \omega (\xi ).\]

See, e.g., (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 6) and references. This says concretely that for each \(\phi \in C_c^\infty(\mathcal{G})\) and Haar measure \(d x\) on \(\mathfrak{g}\), the operator \(\int_{x \in \mathfrak{g}} \phi(x) \pi(\exp(x)) \, d x\) on \(\pi\) belongs to the trace class and has trace \(\int_{\xi \in \mathcal{O}_\pi} (\int_{x \in \mathfrak{g}} \phi(x) \mathop{\mathrm{jac}}(x)^{-1/2} e^{\langle x, \xi \rangle} \, d x) \, d \omega (\xi )\).

§6.5. Construction of \(\mu_\pi\)

Let \(\pi \in A_0\) with \(\lambda_\pi < 0\). Then \(\pi \cong \pi(t,\varepsilon)\) with \(t = \sqrt{-\lambda_\pi} > 0\). Recall that we have chosen a unit vector \(\varphi_\pi \in \pi\) invariant by \(K^1\). The microlocal lift \(\mu_\pi\) of \(\pi\) is defined on \(K\)-finite smooth functions \(\Psi : \Gamma \backslash G \rightarrow \mathbb{C}\) as follows. Set \(\varphi_0 := \varphi_\pi\) and \(s := 1/2 + i t\). Define \(\varphi_q\) for \(q \in \mathbb{Z}\) recursively by the formulas \(i X \varphi_q = (s + q) \varphi_{q+1}\) and \(i Y \varphi_q = (s - q) \varphi_{q-1}\). Then \[\mu_\pi(\Psi) := \sum_{q \in \mathbb{Z}} \langle \varphi_0 \Psi, \varphi_q \rangle.\]

§6.6. Branching coefficients

Let \(\pi, \sigma \in A_0\).

Lemma 12. If \(\sigma\) is not even, then \(\langle \varphi_1 \Psi, \varphi_2 \rangle = 0\) for all \(\varphi_1, \varphi_2 \in \pi\) and \(\Psi \in \sigma\). In particular, \(\mu_\pi(\Psi) = 0\).

We give the proof below after some otherwise relevant preliminaries.

Assume temporarily that for each \(p \in S\), the involutory Hecke operator \(T_{p}\) acts trivially (i.e., with eigenvalue \(+1\) rather than \(-1\)) on \(\sigma\). The triple product formula (Ichino 2008) then implies that for eigenfunctions \(\varphi_1, \varphi_2 \in \pi\) and \(\Psi \in \sigma\), \[\label{eq:branching-coeff-defn}\tag{6.2} |\langle \varphi_1 \Psi, \varphi_2 \rangle|^2 = \mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma) \int_{g \in G} \langle g \varphi_1, \varphi_1 \rangle \overline{\langle g \varphi_2, \varphi_2 \rangle} \langle g \Psi, \Psi \rangle \, d g,\] where \(\mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma)\) is nonnegative real given explicitly in terms of special values of \(L\)-functions; in particular, \[\label{eq:defn-cal-L}\tag{6.3} \mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma) \asymp \frac{L(\pi \otimes \overline{\pi } \otimes \sigma, \tfrac{1}{2})}{ L(\mathop{\mathrm{ad}}\pi,1)^2 L(\mathop{\mathrm{ad}}\sigma,1)},\] where \(L(\dotsb)\) denotes the finite part of an \(L\)-function.

Proof of Lemma 1. Since the distributions \(\mu_\pi\) are invariant by the involutory Hecke operators \(T_p\) (\(p \in S\)), the conclusion is clear if some such operator acts nontrivially on \(\sigma\), so suppose otherwise that each such operator acts trivially. The global root number of \(\sigma\) is then the same as the local root number at the distinguished real place \(\mathfrak{q}\), which, by hypothesis, is \(-1\). Therefore \(L(\sigma, \tfrac{1}{2} ) = 0\). Since \(L(\pi \otimes \overline{\pi } \otimes \sigma, \tfrac{1}{2} ) = L(\mathop{\mathrm{ad}}\pi \otimes \sigma, \tfrac{1}{2} ) L(\sigma, \tfrac{1}{2} )\), we have also \(\mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma) = 0\). The conclusion follows now from \((6.2)\). ◻

Lemma 13. Let \(\sigma \in A_0\) be fixed and even. Let \(\pi\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent element of \(A_0\) with \(\lambda_\pi < 0\) and \(|{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi| \asymp 1\).

  1. Let \(\Psi \in \sigma\) be a fixed eigenfunction. Then \[\label{eq:WW-upper-bound}\tag{6.4} |\mu_\pi(\Psi)|^2 \ll \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma).\]

  2. There is a fixed eigenfunction \(\Psi \in \sigma\) so that \[\label{eq:WW-lower-bound}\tag{6.5} |\mu_\pi(\Psi)|^2 \gg \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma).\]

  3. We have \[\label{eq:convexity-bound}\tag{6.6} \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma) \ll 1.\]

Proof. We may assume that \(\Psi\) is a \(K^1\)-eigenvector. There are two cases:

  • \(\sigma\) is a principal series representation \(\pi(t,\varepsilon)\). Our assumption that \(\sigma\) is even then implies that \(\varepsilon= 1\).

  • \(\sigma\) is a discrete series representation \(\pi(k)\).

Explicit formulas for the matrix coefficient integral of \((6.2)\) in terms of \(\Gamma\)-factors follow from work of Watson (Watson 2002) and Ichino (Ichino 2008) in the first case and from work of Woodbury (Sarnak and Zhao 2019, Appendix) in the second case. Applying Stirling’s asymptotics to these formulas gives the upper bound \((6.4)\). For the lower bound \((6.5)\), we choose \(\Psi\) to be a \(K^1\)-eigenvector of smallest nonnegative weight and appeal again to the explicit formulas. The final estimate \((6.6)\) follows from \((6.5)\) and the trivial bound \(\mu_\pi(\Psi) \ll 1\). ◻

Remark 6. Since we require here estimates rather than explicit formulas, we sketch an alternative proof of Lemma 2. Using \((6.2)\), we may write \(|\mu_\pi(\Psi)|^2 = \mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma) |\mu_\pi^{\mathop{\mathrm{loc}}}(\Psi)|^2\), say. One can show by arguments as in §8.2 and (Nelson 2018, sec. 6.3) that the leading order asymptotics as \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\rightarrow 0\) of \(|\mu_\pi^{\mathop{\mathrm{loc}}}(\Psi)|^2\) are given by a constant multiple of \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\int_{s \in H} \langle s \Psi, \Psi \rangle \, d s\). As in (Michel and Venkatesh 2010, sec. 3.3.1), we may write \(\int_{s \in H} \langle s \Psi, \Psi \rangle \, d s \asymp |\ell(\Psi)|^2\), where \(\ell\) is described in the Kirillov model \(\Psi \mapsto W_{\Psi}\) of \(\sigma\) (with respect to some fixed nontrivial character) by the absolutely convergent integral \(\ell(\Psi) = \int_{y \in \mathbb{R}^\times} W_\Psi(y) \frac{d y}{|y|}\). Thus \(|\mu_\pi^{\mathop{\mathrm{loc}}}(\Psi)|^2 \ll \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\); moreover, if \(\ell(\Psi) \neq 0\), then we can replace “\(\ll\)” with “\(\asymp\)”.

§6.7. Operator calculus

In this subsection, we recall some properties of the operator calculus developed in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021) (and refined in (Nelson 2023, 2021a), but we do not require the refinements). We denote by \(\pi\) an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent unitary representation of \(G\) and by \(\pi^{\infty}\) its subspace of smooth vectors.

§6.7.1. The basic operator assignment

We fix once and for all a cutoff \(\chi \in C_c^\infty(\mathfrak{g})\) with the following properties:

  • The support of \(\chi\) is sufficiently small.

  • \(\chi\) is \([0,1]\)-valued, \(\chi(-x) = \chi(x)\), and \(\chi = 1\) in a neighborhood of the origin.

For any \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent Schwartz function \(a \in \mathcal{S}(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge)\), we may define the following objects (see (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 2) for details):

  • \(a^\vee : \mathfrak{g} \rightarrow \mathbb{C}\), the inverse Fourier transform of \(a\).

  • \(a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} : \mathfrak{g}^\wedge \rightarrow \mathbb{C}\) the \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent function given by rescaling: \(a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(\xi) := a(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\xi)\).

  • \(a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee : \mathfrak{g} \rightarrow \mathbb{C}\), the inverse Fourier transform of the rescaling, thus \(a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee(x) = {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-3} a^\vee(x/\mathop{\mathrm{h}})\).

  • \(\chi a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee \in C_c^\infty(\mathfrak{g})\), the cutoff of \(a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee\).

  • The compactly-supported smooth distribution \(\chi(x) a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee (x) \, d x\) on \(\mathfrak{g}\), which is supported near the origin.

  • The pushforward under the exponential map \(x \mapsto g = \exp(x)\) of this distribution, which may be written \(\widetilde{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)(g) \, d g\) for some \(\widetilde{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) \in C_c^\infty(G)\) supported near the identity; explicitly, \(\widetilde{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)(\exp(x)) = {\mathop{\mathrm{jac}}}^{-1}(x) \chi(x) a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee(x)\).

  • An \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent integral operator \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a:\pi)\) on \(\pi^\infty\), abbreviated \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)\) when \(\pi\) is clear by context, given by \[{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a:\pi) := \pi( \widetilde{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) ) = \int_{x \in \mathfrak{g}} \chi(x) a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee(x) \pi(\exp(x)) \, d x.\]

§6.7.2. Adjoints

The operator \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)\) extends to a bounded operator on \(\pi\) with adjoint \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(\overline{a})\). In particular, if \(a\) is real-valued, then \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)\) is self-adjoint and \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)^2\) is positive-definite.

§6.7.3. Symbol classes

For \(\xi\) belonging to any normed space (e.g., \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\)), we set \(\langle \xi \rangle := (1 + |\xi|^2)^{1/2}\).

For fixed \(0 \leqslant\delta < 1/2\) and \(m \in \mathbb{Z}\), we write \(S^m_\delta\) (denoted “\(S^m[{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta]\)” in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 4)) for the space of \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent functions \(a : \mathfrak{g}^\wedge \rightarrow \mathbb{C}\) such that for each fixed multi-index \(\alpha \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}^{\dim(\mathfrak{g})}\), the corresponding partial derivative \(\partial^{\alpha} a\) enjoys for each \(\xi \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge\) the upper bound \[\partial^\alpha a(\xi) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-\delta |\alpha|} \langle \xi \rangle^{m-|\alpha|}.\] (The implied constant is thus allowed to depend upon \(\alpha\), but not upon \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\) or \(\xi\).) We extend the definition to \(m = \infty\) or \(m = -\infty\) by taking unions or intersections. For instance, an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-independent Schwartz function defines an element of \(S^{-\infty}_\delta\), while a polynomial of fixed degree \(m \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\) and coefficients \(\operatorname{O}(1)\) defines an element of \(S^m_\delta\). Elements of \(S^{-\infty}_\delta\) are in particular \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent Schwartz functions on \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\), so the operators \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) := {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a:\pi)\) may be defined as above.

§6.7.4. Smoothing operators

For Lie algebra elements \(x_1,\dotsc,x_m\), we write \(x_1 \dotsb x_m\) for their product in the universal enveloping algebra and abbreviate \(\pi(x_1 \dotsb x_m) := d \pi(x_1) \dotsb d \pi(x_m)\). We denote by \(\Psi^{-\infty} := \Psi^{-\infty}(\pi)\) the space of \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent operators \(T\) on \(\pi^\infty\) with the property that for any fixed collection \(x_1,\dotsc,x_m,y_1,\dotsc,y_n \in \mathfrak{g}\), the operator norm of \(\pi(x_1 \dotsb x_m) T \pi(y_1 \dotsb y_n)\) is \(\operatorname{O}(1)\). This is easily seen to be equivalent to the definition of (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 3). It is verified in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 12.3) (see part (iii) of Theorem 9) that if \(\pi\) is irreducible, then \[\label{eq:smoothing-ops-have-controlled-trace-norm}\tag{6.7} T \in \Psi^{-\infty} \implies \text{ the trace norm of $T$ is $\operatorname{O}(1)$}.\]

Given an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent scalar \(c\) and vector space \(V\) consisting of \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent quantities, we denote by \(c V\) the vector space of \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent quantities of the form \(c v\), with \(v \in V\). We write \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty V\) for the intersection of \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\eta V\) taken over all fixed \(\eta \in \mathbb{R}\). In particular, we may define \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty \Psi^{-\infty}\); we will regard it as the space of “negligible” operators on \(\pi^\infty\).

§6.7.5. Composition

For \(\phi_1, \phi_2 \in C_c^\infty(\mathfrak{g})\) supported near the origin, let \(\phi_1 \star \phi_2 \in C_c^\infty(\mathfrak{g})\) denote the function for which the distribution \((\phi_1 \star \phi_2)(x) \, d x\) on \(\mathfrak{g}\) is the pullback of the convolution on \(G\) of the images under pushforward of the distributions \(\phi_1(x) \, d x\) and \(\phi_2(x) \, d x\) on \(\mathfrak{g}\). For \(a, b \in S^{-\infty}_{\delta}\), it is verified in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 2.5, §4.6) that the (rescaled) star product \(a \star_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} b\), characterized by the identity \((a \star_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} b)_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} = (\chi a_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee \star \chi b_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee)^\wedge\), defines an element of \(S^{-\infty}_\delta\) that enjoys the composition formula \[\label{eq:composition-formula-for-opp}\tag{6.8} {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(b) \equiv {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a \star_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} b) \pmod{{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty \Psi^{-\infty}}.\] The failure of \((6.8)\) to be an equality is an artefact of the cutoff \(\chi\).

§6.7.6. Equivariance

It follows from (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 5.5) that for \(g \in G\) belonging to a fixed compact subset, \[\label{eq:opp-equivariance}\tag{6.9} {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(g \cdot a) \equiv \pi(g) {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) \pi(g)^{-1} \pmod{{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty \Psi^{-\infty}},\] where \(g \cdot a(\xi) := a(g^{-1} \cdot \xi)\). The error comes from the failure of the cutoff \(\chi\) to be exactly \(G\)-invariant. It will be convenient to assume that \(\chi\) is exactly \(K\)-invariant (by averaging a given cutoff, for instance). We then have \[\label{eq:exact-equivariance-K}\tag{6.10} {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(g \cdot a) = \pi(g) {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) \pi(g)^{-1} \text{ for all } g \in K.\] More precisely, \(\widetilde{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(g \cdot a)\) is the conjugate by \(g\) of \(\widetilde{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)\).

§6.7.7. Star product extension and asymptotics

It is shown in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 4.6) that the star product extends to a compatible family of maps \(\star_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} : S^m_\delta \times S^n_\delta \rightarrow S^{m+n}_\delta\) enjoying the asymptotic expansion: for fixed \(J \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\), \[\label{eq:asymp-expn-star-prod}\tag{6.11} a \star_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} b \equiv \sum_{0 \leqslant j < J} {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^j a \star^j b \pmod{{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{(1-2 \delta ) J} S_{\delta}^{m+n-J}},\] with \(\star^j\) a fixed polynomial-coefficient differential operator, of order \(j\) in each variable, homogeneous of degree \(j\), satisfying the mapping property \(\star^j : S^{m}_\delta \times S^{n}_\delta \rightarrow {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{- 2 \delta j} S^{m+n-j}_\delta\) and given in the simplest case by \(a \star^0 b = a b\).

§6.7.8. Extended operator assignment

It is shown in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 5.6) that \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}\) extends to a compatible family of maps \[{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} : S^m_\delta \rightarrow \{\text{$\mathop{\mathrm{h}}$-dependent operators on $\pi^\infty$}\}\] for which the composition and equivariance properties \((6.8)\), \((6.9)\) and thus \((6.10)\) remain valid.

§6.7.9. Polynomial symbols

It is verified in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 5.2) that if \(p \in S^m_\delta\) is an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent polynomial function (corresponding to some \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent element of \(\mathop{\mathrm{Sym}}(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C})\)), then \[\label{eq:Opp-polynomial-symb}\tag{6.12} {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(p) = \pi(\mathop{\mathrm{sym}}(p_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}})),\] where \(\mathop{\mathrm{sym}}\) denotes the symmetrization map from \(\mathop{\mathrm{Sym}}(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C})\) to the the universal enveloping algebra of \(\mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C}\) and (as above) \(p_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(\xi) = p(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\xi)\).

§6.7.10. Trace estimates

It is shown in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 12.3) that if \(\pi\) is irreducible and tempered (so that the coadjoint orbit \(\mathcal{O}_{\pi}\) as well as its rescaling \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi\) may be defined), then for \(a \in S^{-2}_\delta\), the operator \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)\) is trace-class, with trace asymptotics described for each fixed \(J \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\) by \[\label{eq:kirillov-expanded}\tag{6.13} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)) = \sum_{0 \leqslant j < J} {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^j \int_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi } \mathcal{D}_j a + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{(1-\delta) J}),\] where \(\mathcal{D}_j\) is a fixed constant coefficient differential operators of pure degree \(j\), with \(\mathcal{D}_0 a = a\). In particular, \[\label{eq:kirillov-expanded-2}\tag{6.14} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)) \ll 1.\]

§6.7.11. Clean-up

It follows from (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 10.3) that if \(\pi\) is irreducible (so that its infinitesimal character \(\lambda_\pi \in \mathbb{R}\) may be defined) and \(a \in S^{\infty}_\delta\) has the property that the image under the invariant polynomial \(\Lambda : \mathfrak{g}^\wedge \rightarrow \mathbb{R}\) (see §6.1) of the support of \(a\) is separated by at least \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1/2-\varepsilon}\) from \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi\) for some fixed \(\varepsilon> 0\), then \[{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) \in \langle \lambda_{\pi} \rangle^{-\infty} {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty \Psi^{-\infty},\] where as usual \(\langle \lambda_\pi \rangle := (1 + |\lambda_\pi|^2)^{1/2}\). In particular, the trace norm of \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)\) is \(\operatorname{O}(\langle \lambda_{\pi} \rangle^{-\infty} {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty)\).

(We note a potential point of notational confusion: the rescaled infinitesimal character that we denote here by \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi \in \mathbb{R}\) is written “\(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\lambda_\pi \in [\mathfrak{g}^\wedge] \cong \mathbb{R}\)” in (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021); see (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 9) for details.)

§7. Microlocal lifts

§7.1. Characterizing microlocal lifts via their symmetry

Theorem 3 applies to the distributions \(\Psi \mapsto \mu(\pi(f),\Psi)\) attached to integral operators \(\pi(f)\) with \(f \in C_c^\infty(G)\), but the construction of the microlocal lift \(\mu_\pi\) recorded in §6.5 is in terms of differential operators. We thus encounter the problem of constructing \(\mu_\pi\), or at least some asymptotically equivalent distributions, using integral operators.

We begin with some motivational remarks. Recall the asymptotic notation and terminology set in §1.8. Let \(\pi\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent element of \(A_0\) with \(\lambda_\pi < 0\) and \(|{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi| \asymp 1\). Set \(v := c \sum_{q \in \mathbb{Z} : |q| \leqslant{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-1/2}} \varphi_q \in \pi\), where \(c > 0\) is chosen so that \(v\) is a unit vector, and \(T := v \otimes \overline{v} \in \pi \otimes \overline{\pi}\). It follows from calculations of Wolpert (Wolpert 2001a, sec. 5) (see (Lindenstrauss 2001, sec. 3) for a concise account) that for fixed eigenfunctions \(\Psi\), \[\label{eq:mu-T-Psi-vs-mu-pi-Psi-simple}\tag{7.1} \mu(T,\Psi) = \langle v \Psi, v \rangle = \mu_\pi(\Psi) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1/2}).\] Set \(t := \sqrt{- {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \Omega_\pi } = \sqrt{- {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi } + \operatorname{O}(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}) \asymp 1\). We verify readily that \(\pi(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}X) v = t v + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1/2})\), \(\pi(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}Y) v = t v + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1/2})\) and \(\pi(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}W) v = \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1/2})\); equivalently, for fixed \(Z \in \mathfrak{g}\), we have \(\pi(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}Z) v = Z(\xi(t)) v + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1/2})\), with \(\xi(t) \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge\) as in \((6.1)\); in other words, \(v\) is an approximate eigenvector under the first-order differential operators on \(\pi^\infty\) defined by Lie algebra elements, with eigenvalue described by \(\xi(t)\). We will verify below that some variants of these observations concerning \(v\), phrased in terms of \(T\), give sufficient conditions for (more precise forms of) the estimate \((7.1)\) to hold. Turning to details:

Definition 1. Let \(\pi\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent irreducible unitary representation of \(G\). Let \(T\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent positive-definite trace class operator on \(\pi\) such that \(\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \ll 1\). Let \(\tau\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent element of \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\) with \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau| \ll 1\). Let \(0 < \delta \leqslant 1/2\) be fixed. We say that \(T\) is \(\delta\)-localized at \(\tau\) if for each fixed \(n \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\) and each \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent polynomial function \(p : \mathfrak{g}_\mathbb{C}^* \rightarrow \mathbb{C}\) of degree \(\operatorname{O}(1)\) and coefficients \(\operatorname{O}(1)\) that vanishes to order at least \(n\) at \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau\), we have \[\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(p) T) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{n \delta},\] where \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(p) := {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(p:\pi)\) is as given by \((6.12)\).

One can verify that the \(T\) considered above is \(1/2\)-localized at \(\xi(t)\); we will not need this fact, so we omit the proof.

We may construct integral operators satisfying the above definition:

Lemma 14 (Integral operators attached to localized symbols are localized). Let \(0 < \delta < 1/2\) be fixed. Let \(\tau \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge\) with \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau| \asymp 1\). Let \(a \in {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-\delta} S^{-\infty}_{\delta}\) be real-valued. Let \(\pi\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent tempered irreducible unitary representation of \(G\). Set \(T := \mathop{\mathrm{h}}{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a:\pi)^2\).

Suppose that every element of \(\mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(a) \cap \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi\) is of the form \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta)\). Then \(T\) is \(\delta\)-localized at \(\tau\), and \[\label{eq:trace-estimate-in-localized-case}\tag{7.3} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) = \int_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi} a^2 + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1-\delta}) = \operatorname{O}(1).\]

Proof. We have \(a^2(\xi) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-2 \delta}\). By the lemma of §6.2 and the hypotheses concerning \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau|\) and the support of \(a\), the set \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi \cap \mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(a)\) has symplectic volume \(\operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{2 \delta})\). The required trace estimate \((7.3)\) thus follows from §6.7.10. In particular, the operator \(T\) is positive-definite with \(\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \ll 1\).

To verify the localization property, fix \(n \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\) and let \(p\), as above, be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent polynomial of degree \(\operatorname{O}(1)\) and coefficients \(\operatorname{O}(1)\) that vanishes to order \(\geqslant n\) at \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau\). We must check then that \(\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(p) T) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{n \delta}\).

We pause to observe that for each \(q \in S^{\infty}_\delta\) and each fixed \(J \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\), \[\label{eq:trace-opp-q-T}\tag{7.4} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(q) T ) = \sum_{0 \leqslant j_1, j_2 < J} {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{j_1 + j_2} \int_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi} q \star^{j_1} (a \star^{j_2} a) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{J'}),\] where \(J'\) is fixed and \(J' \rightarrow \infty\) as \(J \rightarrow \infty\). This estimate follows from the composition formula \((6.8)\), the star product asymptotics \((6.11)\) and the trace estimate \((6.13)\), using \((6.7)\) and \((6.14)\) to clean up the remainders. Since \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi \cap \mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(a)\) has symplectic volume \(\operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{2 \delta})\), we have also for fixed \(j_1, j_2 \geqslant 0\) that \[\int_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi} q \star^{j_1} (a \star^{j_2} a) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{2 \delta} \|q \star^{j_1} (a \star^{j_2} a)\|_{L^\infty(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi)}.\]

Returning to the proof of the lemma, choose a ball \(B_1\) with origin \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau\) and radius \(\asymp {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta\) so that \(\mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(a) \cap \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi \subseteq B_1\). Let \(B_2\) denote the ball with the same origin as \(B_1\) but twice the radius. Choose \(\phi \in S^{-\infty}_{\delta}\) taking the value \(1\) on \(B_1\) and the value \(0\) on the complement of \(B_2\). We may then decompose \(p = \phi p + (1 - \phi) p\). We apply the above estimates with \(q =\phi p\) and \(q = (1 - \phi) p\):

  • Our assumptions on \(p\) imply that \(\phi p \in {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{n \delta} S^{-\infty}_\delta\). By \((7.4)\) and the mapping properties of \(\star^j\), the symbol \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{j_1 + j_2} \phi p \star^{j_1} (a \star^{j_2} a)\) belongs to \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-2 \delta + n \delta} S^{-\infty}_\delta\) and thus has \(L^\infty\)-norm \(\operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{- 2 \delta + n \delta})\). It follows that \(\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(\phi p) T) = \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{n \delta} + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{J'})\).

  • By construction, \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi \cap \mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(1-\phi) \cap \mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(a) = \emptyset\), so \((1 - \phi) p \star^{j_1} (a \star^{j_2} a)\) vanishes identically on \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi\), and thus \(\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}((1-\phi) p T) = \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{J'})\).

We conclude by combining these estimates and taking \(J\) large enough. ◻

We verify next the promised relationship between the above definition and \(\mu_\pi\).

Lemma 15 (Some localized operators define microlocal lifts). Fix a mean-zero even eigenfunction \(\Psi \in \sigma \in A_0\). Let \(\pi\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent element of \(A_0\) such that \(\lambda_\pi < 0\) and \(|{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi| \asymp 1\). Abbreviate \(\mathcal{L} := \mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma)\). Let \(T\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent positive-definite trace class operator on \(\pi\) with \(\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \ll 1\). Set \(\tau := \xi(\sqrt{-\Omega_\pi}) \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge\), so that \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau| \asymp 1\). Fix \(0 < \delta \leqslant 1/2\).

Suppose that \(T\) is \(\delta\)-localized at \(\tau\). Then \[\label{eq:mu-T-Psi-approximates-etc}\tag{7.5} \mu(T,\Psi) = \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \mu_\pi(\Psi) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L} } + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty ).\]

The proof is given in §7.2.

Remark 10. The estimate \((7.5)\) implies in particular that \(\mu(T,\Psi) = \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \mu_\pi(\Psi) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta)\), but this weaker estimate is inadequate for our applications, in which we exploit crucially that \(\mathcal{L}\) is “bounded on average” (see §8.1).

Remark 11. Although Lemma 2 is formulated in terms of \(L\)-values, it does not fundamentally exploit the arithmeticity of \(\Gamma \backslash G\). What matters are the properties of \(\mathcal{L}\) enunciated in §6.6, which make sense for general finite volume quotients (see (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 1.4), (Bernstein and Reznikov 2010)).

Remark 12. Lemma 2 may be used to give a proof of the asymptotic \(H\)-invariance of the measures \(\mu_\pi\), roughly in the spirit of (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 26.5); the relevant observations are that

  • if \(T\) is \(\delta\)-localized at \(\tau\) and \(g \in G\) is fixed, then \(\pi(g) T \pi(g)^{-1}\) is \(\delta\)-localized at \(g \cdot \tau\), and

  • \(H\) centralizes the elements \(\xi(t)\).

§7.2. Calculations with raising and lowering operators

Here we record the proof of Lemma 2 of §7.1. The proof is a bit tedious, but not difficult, and unrelated to the main novelties of this work. It is basically a quantification of the arguments used to prove \((7.1)\). (Indeed, it is instructive to note that \((7.5)\) recovers \((7.1)\).) For these reasons, the reader might wish to skim or skip this section on a first reading.

We recall that our task is to verify, under certain assumptions, the estimate \((7.5)\). We have \(\pi \cong \pi(t,\varepsilon)\) with \(t > 0\). We realize \(\pi(t,\varepsilon)\) as \(L^2(\mathbb{Z})\) as in §6. There is then a unique equivariant (isometric) isomorphism \(j_\pi : L^2(\mathbb{Z}) \rightarrow \pi\) that maps the basis element \(e_0\) to \(\varphi_\pi\). Thus \(\varphi_q\), as in the construction of \(\mu_\pi\), is equal to \(b(q) j_\pi(e_q)\), where \(b(q)\) is defined recursively by \[b(q+1) = b(q) \frac{i}{s+q} \sqrt{q(q+1) - \Omega_\pi}.\] Since \(t \in \mathbb{R}\), we have \(|s+q|^2 = q(q+1) - \Omega_\pi\), and so \(|b(q)| = 1\) for all \(q\). Moreover, since \(t > 0\), we have for fixed \(q\) that \(b(q) = 1 + \operatorname{O}(1/t)\). Thus the vectors \(\varphi_q\) are asymptotically quite close to the \(j_\pi(e_q)\).

By a limiting argument, it will suffice to consider the case that \(T\) is a finite rank operator \(T = \sum_i j_\pi(v_i) \otimes \overline{j_\pi(v_i)}\) attached to some finite orthogonal subset \(\{v_i\}\) of \(L^2(\mathbb{Z})\). For \(q,\xi \in \mathbb{Z}\), we set \(T(q,\xi) := \sum_i v_i(q) \overline{v_i(q+\xi)}\), so that \(T = \sum_{q,\xi} T(q,\xi) j_\pi(e_q) \otimes \overline{j_\pi(e_{q+\xi})}\), and \(\Psi(q,\xi) := \langle j_\pi(e_{q}) \Psi, j_\pi(e_{q+\xi}) \rangle\), so that \[\mu_\pi(\Psi) = \sum_{\xi} b(\xi) \Psi(0,\xi)\] and \[\mu(T,\Psi) = \sum_{q,\xi} T(q,\xi) \Psi(q,\xi)\] and \[\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) = \sum_{i,q} |v_i(q)|^2 = \sum_q T(q,0).\] By Cauchy–Schwarz and the assumed trace estimate for \(T\), \[\sum_q |T(q,\xi)| \leqslant \sum_q \sum_i |v_i(q) \overline{v_i(q + \xi)}| \leqslant\sum_{q,i} |v_i(q)|^2 = \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \ll 1.\]

Set \(r := \sqrt{- \Omega_\pi}\), so that \(\tau = \xi(r)\). We temporarily abbreviate \(X,Y,W := \pi(X), \pi(Y), \pi(W)\). We will use the following consequences of our assumption that \(T\) is \(\delta\)-localized at \(\tau\): \[\label{eqn:W-cond-for-T}\tag{7.6} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}((\mathop{\mathrm{h}}W)^n T) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{n \delta} \text{ for each fixed $n \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}$},\] \[\label{eqn:X-cond-for-T}\tag{7.7} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}((\mathop{\mathrm{h}}X - \mathop{\mathrm{h}}r) T) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta.\] Indeed, we have \(X(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau) = Y(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau) = \mathop{\mathrm{h}}r\) and \(W(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau) = 0\), so the polynomial \(p = W^n\) vanishes to order \(n\) at \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau\) and satisfies \(\mathop{\mathrm{sym}}(p_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}) = (\mathop{\mathrm{h}}W)^n\), while the polynomial \(p = X - \mathop{\mathrm{h}}r\) vanishes to order \(1\) at \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau\) and satisfies \(\mathop{\mathrm{sym}}(p_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}) = \mathop{\mathrm{h}}X - \mathop{\mathrm{h}}r\).

By \((7.6)\) with \(n = 2\), we have \[\sum_{i,q} |\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q|^2 |v_i(q)|^2 = \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}((\mathop{\mathrm{h}}W)^2 T) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{2 \delta}.\] Using Cauchy–Schwarz as above, it follows that for fixed \(\xi\), \[\label{eqn:T-W-decay-bulk}\tag{7.8} \sum_q |T(q,\xi)| \cdot |h q| \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta}.\] We now fix \(0 < \delta ' < \delta\), and argue using \((7.6)\) for arbitrary fixed \(n\) that \[\label{eqn:T-W-decay}\tag{7.9} \sum_{q : |\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q| \geqslant{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta '}} |T(q,\xi)| \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty.\]

We now investigate the consequences of \((7.7)\). We have \[\mathop{\mathrm{h}}X T = \sum_{q,\xi} T(q,\xi) \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sqrt{r^2 + q(q+1)} e_{q + 1} \otimes \overline{e_{q + \xi}},\] thus \[\label{eqn:formula-trace-h-X-T}\tag{7.10} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}X T) = \sum_{q} \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sqrt{r^2 + q(q+1)} T(q,1).\] Our assumptions on \(\pi\) imply that \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}r \asymp 1\). We estimate the latter sum in the range \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q | \geqslant{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta '}\) using \((7.9)\) and in the range \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q | < {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta '}\) using the Taylor expansion \[\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sqrt{r^2 + q(q+1)} = \mathop{\mathrm{h}}r + \operatorname{O}(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q|).\] The contribution to \((7.10)\) of the remainder in this expansion is treated using \((7.8)\). We extend the sum to all \(q\) using \((7.9)\) once again. We obtain in this way that \[\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}X T) = \mathop{\mathrm{h}}{r} \sum_{q} T(q,1) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta).\] Since \(T(q,1) = \sum_i v_i(q) \overline{v_i(q+1)}\), the estimate \((7.7)\) thus translates to \[\label{eqn:X-cond-for-T-2}\tag{7.11} \sum_{q,i} |v_i(q)|^2 = \sum_{q,i} v_i(q) \overline{v_i(q+1)} + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta).\] By iterating this estimate, one may deduce more generally that for each fixed \(\xi\), \[\label{eqn:T-X-cons-0}\tag{7.12} \sum_{q,i} |v_i(q)|^2 = \sum_{q,i} v_i(q) \overline{v_i(q+\xi)} + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta),\] which translates to \[\label{eqn:T-X-cons}\tag{7.13} \sum_q T(q,\xi) = \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta).\] We record details concerning this deduction at the end of §7.2.

Recall that \(\Psi(q,\xi) = 0\) unless \(|\xi| \leqslant C\) for some fixed \(C\). We have the trivial bound \[\label{eqn:trivial-for-Psi-pi}\tag{7.14} |\Psi(q,\xi)| \leqslant\|\Psi \|_{L^\infty(\Gamma \backslash G)} \ll 1\] for all \(q, \xi\). Suppose now that \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q| \leqslant{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta '}\). We claim then that \[\label{eqn:Psi-pi-triv}\tag{7.15} \Psi(q,\xi) \ll \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L} } + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\infty}\] and \[\label{eqn:Psi-pi-lipschitz}\tag{7.16} \Psi(q,\xi) = \Psi(0,\xi) + \operatorname{O}(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q| \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}} + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\infty}).\] We will prove these when \(q \geqslant 0\); an analogous argument applies to negative \(q\).

For \(j \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\) and \(q,\xi \in \mathbb{Z}\), let \(\Psi^j(q,\xi)\) be defined like \(\Psi(q,\xi)\), but with \(\Psi\) replaced with \(X^j \Psi\). We will work in what follows with fixed values of \(j\), so that \(\|X^j \Psi \| \ll 1\).

By \((6.6)\), we have \[\label{eqn:woodbury-stirling}\tag{7.17} \Psi^j(0,\xi) \ll \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L} }.\] We also have the trivial bound \[\label{eqn:trivial-Psi-j}\tag{7.18} \Psi^j(q,\xi) \ll 1,\] as in \((7.14)\).

We now argue recursively using the following instance of “partial integration”: the integral over \(\Gamma \backslash G\) of \(X (j_\pi(e_q) \overline{j_\pi(e_{q+\xi})} X^j \Psi)\) vanishes. Expanding this out, we obtain with \(f(q) := \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sqrt{r^2 + q(q+1)}\) that \[f(q+\xi) \Psi^j(q,\xi) = f(q) \Psi^j(q+1,\xi) + \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\Psi^{j+1}(q,\xi+1).\] For \(q\) in the indicated range and \(\xi \ll 1\), we have \(f(q) \asymp 1\) and \(f(q+\xi) = f(q) + \operatorname{O}(\mathop{\mathrm{h}})\). Hence for such \(q\) and \(\xi\), \[\label{eqn:Psi-pi-j-main-recursive-bound}\tag{7.19} \Psi^j(q+1,\xi) - \Psi^j(q,\xi) \ll \mathop{\mathrm{h}}( \left\lvert \Psi^j(q,\xi) \right\rvert + \left\lvert \Psi^j(q+1,\xi) \right\rvert + \left\lvert \Psi^{j+1}(q,\xi+1) \right\rvert ).\]

Fix \(J \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\) and then \(C \in \mathbb{R}_{\geqslant 1}\) sufficiently large, and set \[\beta_j(q) := C (1 + C \mathop{\mathrm{h}})^{-q} \sup_{\xi} |\Psi^j(q,\xi)|.\] We consider \(q \geqslant 0\) with \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q| \leqslant{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta '}\). Having chosen \(C\) large enough, the estimate \((7.19)\) implies \[\label{eqn:recursion-for-beta-j}\tag{7.20} \beta_j(q + 1) \leqslant\beta_j(q) + C \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\beta_{j+1}(q) \quad (0 \leqslant j < J).\] Similarly, by \((7.18)\), \[\beta_J(q) \leqslant 1.\] Thus the sequence of \((J+1)\)-dimensional row vectors \[\beta(q) := (\beta_0(q), \beta_1(q), \dotsc, \beta_{J-1}(q), 1)\] satisfy \[\beta(q) \leqslant\beta(0) M^q, \quad M := \begin{pmatrix} 1 & & & & \\ C \mathop{\mathrm{h}}& 1 & & \\ & \dotsb & \dotsb & & \\ & & \dotsb & 1 & \\ & & & C \mathop{\mathrm{h}}& 1 \end{pmatrix}.\] We also have, by \((7.17)\) and the estimate \((1 + C \mathop{\mathrm{h}})^q = 1 + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta '})\), the initial bound \[\beta_j(0) \ll \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}} \quad (0 \leqslant j < J).\] These lead to \[\beta_j(q) \ll \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L} } + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{(J-j) \delta}.\] Taking \(j = 0\) and recalling that \(J\) was arbitrary, we obtain \((7.15)\), and also its analogue for \(\Psi^1\); inserting the latter into the \(j=0\) case of \((7.19)\) then gives \((7.16)\).

We now combine the above estimates to conclude. Expanding the definitions, we have \[\mu(T,\Psi) = \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \mu_{\pi}(\Psi) + S_1 + S_2 + S_3\] where \[S_1 := \sum_{\xi} \left(\sum_q T(q,\xi) - \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T)\right) \Psi(0,\xi),\] \[S_2 := \sum_{q,\xi} T(q,\xi) (\Psi(q,\xi) - \Psi(0,\xi)),\] \[S_3 := \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \sum_{\xi} (1 - b(\xi)) \Psi(0,\xi).\] Using \((7.15)\) and \((7.13)\), we see that \(S_1 \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L} } + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty\). To bound \(S_2\), we estimate the contribution from \(|\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q| \geqslant{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta '}\) via \((7.9)\) and \((7.15)\). We then estimate the remaining contribution via \((7.16)\). We obtain \[S_2 \ll \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L} } \sum_{\xi : |\xi| \leqslant C} \sum_{q} |T(q,\xi)| \cdot |\mathop{\mathrm{h}}q| + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\infty} \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta} \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L} } + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty.\] For \(S_3\), we use that \(\Psi(0,\xi) \ll \sqrt{h \mathcal{L}}\) and that \(\Psi(0,\xi) \neq 0\) only if \(|\xi| = \operatorname{O}(1)\), in which case \(b(\xi) = 1 + \operatorname{O}(\mathop{\mathrm{h}})\); thus \(S_3 \ll \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}}\). This completes the proof of \((7.5)\).

We finally record the promised details concerning the deduction of \((7.12)\). Let \((V, \langle , \rangle)\) be a complex inner product space, with unitary group \(\mathop{\mathrm{U}}(V)\).

Lemma 19. For \(a, b \in \mathop{\mathrm{U}}(V)\) and \(v \in V\), we have \[\lvert \langle a b v - v, v \rangle \rvert^{1/2} \leqslant\lvert \langle a v - v, v \rangle \rvert^{1/2} + \lvert \langle b v - v, v \rangle \rvert ^{1/2}.\]

Proof. Introduce the abbreviations \[\varepsilon_a := \lvert \langle a v - v, v \rangle \rvert^{1/2}, \quad \varepsilon_b := \lvert \langle b v - v, v \rangle \rvert^{1/2}.\] We observe first, by expanding the square and invoking the assumed unitarity, that \[\label{eqn:expand-square-avminusv}\tag{7.26} \lVert a v - v \rVert^2 = 2 \mathrm{Re}\langle v - a v, v \rangle \leqslant 2 \varepsilon_a^2,\] and similarly for \(b\). Consider now the inner product \[I := \langle a (b v - v), a v - v \rangle.\] On the one hand, by Cauchy–Schwarz and \((7.26)\), we have \(|I| \leqslant 2 \varepsilon_a \varepsilon_b\). On the other hand, by expanding out and invoking the unitarity of \(a\) and \(b\), we see that \[\langle a b v - v, v \rangle = \langle a v - v , v \rangle + \langle b v - v, v \rangle - I,\] and thus \[|\langle a b v - v, v \rangle| \leqslant\varepsilon_a^2 + \varepsilon_b^2 + 2 \varepsilon_a \varepsilon_b = (\varepsilon_a + \varepsilon_b)^2.\] The required inequality follows. ◻

Corollary 1. For \(a \in \mathop{\mathrm{U}}(V)\), \(v \in V\) and \(n \in \mathbb{Z}\), we have \[\label{eq:lvertlangle-an-v}\tag{7.27} \lvert\langle a^n v - v, v \rangle \rvert \leqslant n^2 \lvert \langle a v - v, v \rangle \rvert.\]

Proof. It suffices to consider the case \(n \geqslant 0\); otherwise, replace \((n,a)\) with \((-n, a^{-1})\) and use that \[\langle a v - v, v \rangle = \overline{\langle a^{-1} v - v, v \rangle}.\] The required inequality is clear when \(n=0,1\). For \(n \geqslant 2\), we induct; the induction step is given by the lemma with \(b := a^{n-1}\). ◻

Remark 7. By taking \(V\) to be \(\mathbb{C}^2\) with the standard inner product and \(a\) to be rotation by some small angle, we may see that \((7.27)\) is sharp, i.e., the factor \(n^2\) cannot be replaced by anything smaller.

We now explain how the corollary implies \((7.12)\). Let \(V\) be the inner product space consisting of collections of complex numbers \(v = \{v_i(q)\}_{i,q}\), where \(i\) ranges over some index set \(I\) and \(q\) ranges over \(\mathbb{Z}\), with the inner product given by \[\langle u, v \rangle = \sum_{i,q} u_i(q) \overline{v_i(q)}.\] Let \(a\) denote the unitary operator on \(V\) given by \[(a v)_i(q) = v_i(q+1).\] Our hypothesis \((7.11)\) then reads \(\langle v, v \rangle = \langle v, a v \rangle + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta)\), while the desired conclusion \((7.12)\) reads \(\langle v, a^{\xi} v \rangle = \langle v, v \rangle + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta)\) for fixed \(\xi\). Since \(\xi^2 = \operatorname{O}(1)\), the required implication follows immediately from \((7.27)\).

§7.3. Constructing microlocal lifts via integral operators

Recall the coordinates \(\xi = i \begin{pmatrix} \xi_1 & \xi_3 \\ \xi_2 & - \xi_1 \end{pmatrix}\) on \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\). We henceforth fix some \(0 < \delta < 1/2\). We denote by \(\tilde{\mathcal{K}}\) the set of all real-valued \(a \in {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-\delta} S^{-\infty}_\delta\) with the following properties:

  • \(a (\xi) = 0\) unless \(\xi_1 > 0\) and \(\xi_1 \asymp 1\) and \(\xi_2,\xi_3 \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta\); equivalently, \(a\) is supported in a \(\operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta)\) neighborhood of some fixed compact subset of \(\{\xi(t) : t > 0\}\). In particular, \(a\) vanishes identically on \(\mathcal{O}(\lambda)\) unless \(\lambda < 0\) and \(|\lambda| \asymp 1\).

  • We have \[\label{eq:Weyl-negates-symbol}\tag{7.28} a(-(w \cdot \xi)) = a(\xi)\] for all \(\xi \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge\), where \(w = \begin{pmatrix} 0 & 1 \\ -1 & 0 \end{pmatrix} \in G\). Equivalently, \(a(\xi)\) is invariant under swapping the coordinates \(\xi_2\) and \(\xi_3\).

To each \(a \in \tilde{\mathcal{K}}\), we attach an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent element \(k\) of \(C_c^\infty(\mathbb{R}_{<0})\) by the formula \[k(\lambda) := \int_{\mathcal{O}(\lambda)} a^2.\] We note that the support condition for \(k\) follows from that for \(a\). We denote by \(\mathcal{K}\) the set of \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent functions \(k\) arising in this way. We verify readily that \(\mathcal{K}\) has the “control” and “richness” properties enunciated in §1.8.

We henceforth work with one such \(k\) together with a corresponding symbol \(a \in \tilde{\mathcal{K}}\). We fix \(C \geqslant 10\) sufficiently large that \(a (\xi) = 0\) unless \(3/C < \xi_1 < C/3\). Then \(k(\lambda) = 0\) unless \(2/C < \sqrt{-\lambda} < C/2\).

We say that an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent irreducible unitary representation \(\pi\) of \(G\) is good if \(\lambda_\pi < 0\) and \(1/C \leqslant\sqrt{-{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi} \leqslant C\), and otherwise that \(\pi\) is bad. Informally, the bad \(\pi\) are those whose rescaled infinitesimal characters are sufficiently separated from the support of \(a\) that they play little role in our analysis.

Lemma 20. Let \(\pi\) be an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent irreducible unitary representation of \(G\). Set \[T := \mathop{\mathrm{h}}{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)^2,\] where \({\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) := {\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a:\pi)\).

  1. \(T\) defines a positive-definite trace-class operator on \(\pi\). If \(\pi\) is good, then \[\label{eq:trace-estimate-good-case}\tag{7.29} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) = k({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1-\delta}).\] If \(\pi\) is bad, then \[\label{eq:bad-trace-pi-estimate}\tag{7.30} \mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) = \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty \langle \lambda_\pi \rangle^{-\infty}).\]

  2. Fix a mean-zero even eigenfunction \(\Psi \in \sigma \in A_0\), and suppose that \(\pi \in A_0\). If \(\pi\) is good, then \[\label{eq:compare-with-microlocal}\tag{7.31} \mu(T,\Psi) = k({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi) \mu_\pi(\Psi) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta} \sqrt{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}} + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty),\] where \(\mathcal{L} := \mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma)\). If \(\pi\) is bad, then \[\label{eq:bad-pi-estimate-for-T-pi-Psi}\tag{7.32} \mu(T,\Psi) = \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty \langle \lambda_\pi \rangle^{-\infty}).\]

Proof. If \(\pi\) is bad, then the estimate \((7.30)\) follows from §6.7.11, while \((7.32)\) follows from \((7.30)\) and the inequality \(|\mu(T,\Psi)| \leqslant\mathop{\mathrm{trace}}(T) \|\Psi\|_{L^\infty}\). Suppose that \(\pi\) is good. Then \(\pi\) is tempered, \(\lambda_\pi < 0\) and \(|{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi| \asymp 1\). Moreover, every element of \(\mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(a) \cap \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{O}_\pi\) is of the form \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\tau + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta)\) with \(\tau = \xi(t)\), \(t = \sqrt{- \Omega_\pi}\). The hypotheses of \((7.3)\) are thus satisfied, while the conclusion gives \((7.30)\). To deduce \((7.31)\), we combine the lemmas of §7.1. ◻

§8. Proofs of main results

Following the sketch of §1.10, we must establish the three key estimates \((1.19)\), \((1.20)\) and \((1.21)\). These are recalled and verified in the following subsections, followed by the modifications needed for the holomorphic case.

§8.1. Quantum variance sums via integral operators

Recall from §7.3 that we have chosen \(k \in \mathcal{K}\) and a corresponding symbol \(a \in \tilde{\mathcal{K}}\). Then \[f := {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{3/2} \widetilde{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a) \ast \widetilde{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a)\] (here \(\ast\) denotes convolution in \(C_c^\infty(G)\) with respect to our chosen Haar measure) is an \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-dependent positive-definite element of \(C_c^\infty(G)\), supported in a fixed small neighborhood of the identity element. For each unitary representation \(\pi\) of \(G\), we have \(\pi(f) = {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1/2} T_\pi\) with \(T_\pi := \mathop{\mathrm{h}}{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a:\pi)^2\) as in the lemma of §7.3. Recall from §1.8 that \[\label{eq:recap-defn-cal-V}\tag{8.1} \mathcal{V}(f) = \sum_{\pi \in A_0} \iota_{\pi} \mu(\pi(f),\Psi_1) \overline{\mu(\pi(f),\Psi_2)} = \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sum_{\pi \in A_0} \iota_\pi \mu(T_\pi, \Psi_1) \overline{ \mu(T_\pi, \Psi_2) }.\] The purpose of this section is to verify the claimed estimate \((1.19)\) relating \(\mathcal{V}(f)\) to the quantum variance of microlocal lifts, which we copy here for convenience: \[\mathcal{V}(f) = \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sum_{\pi \in A_0} \iota_{\pi} k({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi)^2 \mu_\pi(\Psi_1) \overline{\mu_\pi(\Psi_2)} + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta). \tag{\ref{eqn:req-V}}\] We begin with an a priori bound:

Lemma 21. Fix \(C \geqslant 1\), and fix an even \(\sigma \in A_0\). Then \[\label{eq:SZ-upper-bound-2}\tag{8.2} {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \sum_{ \substack{ \pi \in A_0 : \\ C^{-1} \leqslant- {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi \leqslant C } } \iota_{\pi} \mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma) \ll 1.\]

Proof. This can be deduced using an approximate functional equation and the Kuznetsov formula as in the work of Luo–Sarnak–Zhao (who in fact obtain and require asymptotic formulas with strong error terms rather than merely upper bounds \((8.2)\) of the expected order of magnitude). For completeness, we record a self-contained proof of \((8.2)\). We assume \(k \in \mathcal{K}\) chosen so that \(k(\lambda) \geqslant 1\) whenever \(C^{-1} \leqslant-\lambda \leqslant C\). Let \(\pi\) be as in \((8.2)\), so that \(|k({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi)|^2 \geqslant 1\). Set \(T_\pi := \mathop{\mathrm{h}}{\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}}_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(a:\pi)^2\). Fix an eigenfunction \(\Psi \in \sigma\) for which \((6.5)\) holds, so that \(|k({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi)|^2 |\mu_\pi(\Psi)|^2 \gg \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma)\). It follows then by \((7.31)\) that \(|\mu(T_\pi,\Psi)|^2 \gg \mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma)\). Thus the left hand side of \((8.2)\) is bounded by a fixed multiple of \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sum_{\pi \in A_0 } \iota_{\pi} |\mu(T_\pi,\Psi)|^2\), which is just \(\mathcal{V}(f)\) specialized to \(\Psi_1 = \Psi_2 = \Psi\). The identity \((1.13)\) and the estimates \((1.20)\) and \((1.21)\) give an asymptotic formula for \(\mathcal{V}(f)\) which implies in particular that \(\mathcal{V}(f) \ll 1\). This completes the proof. (We note that the proofs of the estimates \((1.20)\) and \((1.21)\), given below, do not depend upon the lemma that we are proving, so our argument is non-circular.) ◻

We now verify \((1.19)\). The contribution from bad \(\pi\) to \((8.1)\) is adequately estimated using \((7.32)\) and the very weak Weyl law \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{100} \sum_{\pi \in A_0} \iota_\pi \langle \lambda_\pi \rangle^{-10} \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{10}\), say. If \(\pi\) is good, then we see by \((6.4)\) and \((7.31)\) that \[\label{eqn:approx-for-mu-mu}\tag{8.3} \begin{split} \mu(T_\pi,\Psi_1) \overline{\mu(T_\pi,\Psi_2)} &= k({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^2 \lambda_\pi)^2 \mu_\pi(\Psi_1) \overline{\mu_\pi(\Psi_2)} \\ &\quad + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1+\delta} \sqrt{\mathcal{L}_1 \mathcal{L}_2} + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\infty}) \end{split}\] where \(\mathcal{L}_j := \mathcal{L}(\pi,\sigma_j)\). To discard the error, we apply Cauchy–Schwarz followed by the above lemma, which gives for \(j=1,2\) that \[\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\sum_{\text{good }\pi \in A_0} \iota_{\pi} ({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1+\delta} \mathcal{L}_j + {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\infty) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta.\] The proof of \((1.19)\) is now complete. In the following sections we will verify \((1.20)\) and \((1.21)\), thereby completing the proof of Theorem 1.

We note for future reference that for \(x \in \mathcal{G}\) (see §6.4), \[f(\exp(x)) = {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{3/2} {\mathop{\mathrm{jac}}}^{-1/2}(x) b^\vee_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(x),\] where \(b \in {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-\delta} S^{-\infty}_{\delta}\) is characterized by the identity \(b_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\vee} = {\mathop{\mathrm{jac}}}^{-1/2} (a \star_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} a)_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee\). By (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 7.8), it admits an asymptotic expansion \(b \sim \sum_{j \geqslant 0} b_j\) with \(b_0 = a^2\) and \(b_j \in {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-2 \delta + (1 - 2 \delta) j} S^{-\infty}_{\delta}\); by this we mean that \(b - \sum_{0 \leqslant j < J} b_j \in {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{- 2 \delta + (1 - 2 \delta )J} S^{-\infty}_{\delta}\) for each fixed \(J \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\).

§8.2. Main term estimates

Before beginning the proof of \((1.20)\), we establish a relevant integral formula. Recall that we have fixed a Haar measure \(d g\) on \(G\). Let \(d x\) denote the compatibly normalized Haar measure on \(\mathfrak{g}\) (§6.4), and let \(d \xi\) denote the corresponding dual measure on \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\), so that for instance \(\phi(0) = \int_{x \in \mathfrak{g}} (\int_{\xi \in \mathfrak{g}^\vee} \phi(\xi) e^{\langle x, \xi \rangle}\, d \xi ) \, dx\) for \(\phi \in C_c^\infty(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge)\). Let \(\Phi \in C_c(G)\) and \(\phi_1,\phi_2 \in C_c(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge)\). The integral \[I := \int_{g \in G} \Phi(g) \int_{\xi \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge} \phi_1(g \cdot \xi) \phi_2(\xi) \, d \xi \, d g\] is then independent of these choices of measure. Our immediate aim is to express \(I\) in terms of the normalized symplectic measures on coadjoint orbits. We do this under the assumption that the \(\phi_j\) are supported on the “negative cone” \(\{\xi : \Lambda(\xi) < 0\} = \cup_{t > 0} \mathcal{O}(-t^2)\), which is the case relevant for our applications.

To state our result requires some notation. Let \(t > 0\). Recall that \(\xi(t)\) (see \((6.1)\)) has \(G\)-orbit \(\mathcal{O}(-t^2)\) and stabilizer \(H\) (the diagonal subgroup). For \(\xi,\eta \in \mathcal{O}(-t^2)\), set \[G_{\xi \leftarrow \eta} := \{g \in G : g \cdot \eta = \xi \}.\] For any choice of elements \(x,y \in G\) with \(x \cdot \xi(t) = \xi\) and \(y \cdot \xi(t) = \eta\), we obtain a bijection \(H \cong G_{\xi \leftarrow \eta}\) given by \(s \mapsto x s y^{-1}\). We equip \(G_{\xi \leftarrow \eta}\) with the transport of the Haar measure on \(H\), as normalized in §1.5. The measure so-defined on \(G_{\xi \leftarrow \eta}\) is independent of the choice of \(x,y\).

Lemma 22. Let \(I\) be as defined above, with \(\phi_1,\phi_2\) supported on \(\{\xi : \Lambda(\xi) < 0\}\). Then \[I = \int_{t > 0} \int_{\xi,\eta \in \mathcal{O}(-t^2)} \phi_1(\xi) \phi_2(\eta) \left(\int_{G_{\xi \leftarrow \eta}} \Phi\right) \, d \omega (\xi ) \, d \omega (\eta ) \, \frac{d t}{2 \pi }.\]

Proof. Although both sides of the identity are independent of all choices of Haar measure, it is convenient to make explicit choices for the proof. Recall the coordinates and notation of §6.1. We assume that \(d x = \frac{1}{ \pi } d x_1 \, d x_2 \, d x_3\). This normalizes a Haar measure \(d g\) on \(G\), as well as the dual measure \(d \xi = \frac{1}{8 \pi^2} d \xi_1 \, d \xi_2 \, d \xi_3\) on \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\). We equip \(G/H\) with the quotient measure \(d g\). We note in passing that \(H\) meets both connected components of \(G\), so the quotient \(G/H\) could be replaced by the quotient \(G^1/H^1\) of connected groups in what follows.

Let \(t > 0\). We first explicate the value \(\omega_{\xi(t)}\) of the canonical \(2\)-form \(\omega\) on \(\mathcal{O}(-t^2)\) (§6.2) at the point \(\xi(t)\). Under the differentiated orbit map \(\mathfrak{g} \rightarrow T_{\xi(t)}(\mathcal{O}(-t^2))\), we have \(e_2 \mapsto - 2 t e_3^*\) and \(e_3 \mapsto 2 t e_2^*\). Thus \(\omega_{\xi(t)}(- 2 t e_3^* \wedge 2 t e_2^*) = \langle \xi(t), [e_2, e_3] \rangle/2 \pi i = 2 t / 2 \pi\), and so \(\omega_{\xi(t)} = \frac{1}{4 \pi t } d \xi_2 \wedge d \xi_3\).

Let \(\beta = \frac{1}{8 \pi^2} d \xi_1 \wedge d \xi_2 \wedge d \xi_3\) denote the differential form on \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\) corresponding to \(d \xi\). Then \(\beta_{\xi(t)} = \frac{t}{2 \pi } d \xi_1 \wedge \omega_{\xi(t)}\). This implies the integral formula \[\int_{\xi \in \mathfrak{g}^\wedge} \phi(\xi) \, d \xi = \int_{t > 0} \left(\int_{\mathcal{O}(-t^2)} \phi \right) \, \frac{t \, d t}{ 2 \pi }\] for \(\phi\) supported on \(\cup_{t>0} \mathcal{O}(-t^2)\).

On the other hand, the Haar measure on \(H\) corresponds to the Haar measure on \(\mathfrak{h} = \mathop{\mathrm{Lie}}(H)\) given by \(d x_1\). The induced quotient measure on \(G/H\) corresponds to the differential form on \(\mathfrak{g}/\mathfrak{h}\) given at the origin by \(\frac{1}{\pi } d x_2 \wedge d x_3\). Under the orbit map isomorphism \(\mathfrak{g}/\mathfrak{h} \cong T_{\xi(t)} \mathcal{O}(-t^2)\), we have \(\frac{1}{ \pi } d x_2 \wedge d x_3 \mapsto \pm \frac{1}{4 \pi t^2} d \xi_2 \wedge d \xi_3 = \pm \frac{1}{t} \omega_{\xi(t)}\). This implies the integral formula \[\int_{\mathcal{O}(-t^2)} \phi = t \int_{g \in G/H} \phi(g \cdot \xi(t)) \, d g.\]

Combining the formulas established thus far, we obtain \[\begin{align} I &= \int_{g \in G} \Phi(g) \int_{t > 0} \int_{\xi \in \mathcal{O}(-t^2)} \phi_1(g \cdot \xi) \phi_2(\xi) \, d \omega (\xi ) \, \frac{t \, d t}{2 \pi } \, d g \\ &= \int_{g \in G} \Phi(g) \int_{t > 0} \int_{y \in G/H} \phi_1(g \cdot \xi(t)) \phi_2(y \cdot \xi(t)) \, d y \, \frac{t^2 \, d t}{2 \pi } \, d g \\ &= \int_{t > 0} \int_{x,y \in G/H} \phi_1(x \cdot \xi(t)) \phi_2(y \cdot \xi(t)) \int_{s \in H} \Phi(x s y^{-1}) \, d s \, d x \, d y \, \frac{t^2 \, d t}{2 \pi }, \end{align}\] which simplifies to the required formula. ◻

We now verify \((1.20)\), which we copy here for convenience: \[\mathcal{I}(f) = \int_{s \in H} \langle s \Psi_1^w, \Psi_2^w \rangle \,d s \int_{t > 0} k(-t^2)^2 \, \frac{d t}{2 \pi} + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta). \tag{\ref{eqn:req-I}}\] Using \((6.10)\) and \((7.28)\), we compute that \(w \cdot f(g) = f(g^{-1})\), hence \(\mathfrak{S} f = \frac{f + w \cdot f}{2}\), and so \[\label{eq:I-f-after-simpl}\tag{8.5} \mathcal{I}(f) = \int_{g \in G} \langle g \cdot f, f \rangle_{G} \Phi(g) \,d g , \quad \Phi(g) := \langle g \Psi_1^w, \Psi_2^w \rangle.\] We compatibly normalize Haar measures on \(G\), \(\mathfrak{g}\) and \(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge\) as above. By change of variables and Parseval, we then have \[\begin{align} \langle g \cdot f, f \rangle_G &= \int_{x \in \mathfrak{g}} \mathop{\mathrm{jac}}(x) f(g^{-1} \exp(x) g ) \overline{f(\exp(x))} \, d x \\ &= {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^3 \langle g \cdot b_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee, b_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\vee \rangle_{\mathfrak{g}} \\ &= \langle g \cdot b, b \rangle_{\mathfrak{g}^\wedge}. \end{align}\]

As explained in §3.1.6, all of these integrals converge absolutely. For instance, we have \(\Phi(g) \ll \|\mathop{\mathrm{Ad}}(g)\|^{-\eta}\) for some fixed \(\eta > 0\) by standard bounds for matrix coefficients (§3.1.5), while \(\langle g \cdot b, b \rangle_{\mathfrak{g}^\wedge} \ll \|\mathop{\mathrm{Ad}}(g)\|^{-1}\) by direct calculation. Since \(\int_{g \in G} \|\mathop{\mathrm{Ad}}(g)\|^{-1-\eta} \,d g < \infty\), the required convergence follows. The same argument gives also that for any \(c_1, c_2 \in S^{-\infty}_{\delta}\), \[\label{eqn:a-priori-g-c1-c2}\tag{8.6} \int_{g \in G} \Phi(g) \langle g \cdot c_1, c_2 \rangle_{\mathfrak{g}^\wedge} \,d g \ll 1.\]

Using \((8.6)\) as an a priori estimate and inserting the asymptotic expansion \(b \sim \sum_{j \geqslant 0} b_j\) noted previously, we deduce that for each fixed \(N \geqslant 0\), there is a fixed \(J \geqslant 0\) so that \[\mathcal{I}(f) = \sum_{0 \leqslant j_1,j_2 < J} I_{j_1,j_2} + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^N), \quad I_{j_1,j_2} := \int_{g \in G} \Phi(g) \langle g \cdot b_{j_1}, b_{j_2} \rangle_{\mathfrak{g}^\wedge} \,d g .\] The above lemma gives, with \(\Phi'(\xi,\eta) := \int_{G_{\xi \leftarrow \eta}} \Phi\), that \[\label{eqn:see-coadjoint-orbits-appear-yay}\tag{8.7} I_{j_1,j_2} = \int_{t > 0} \int_{\xi, \eta \in \mathcal{O}(-t^2)} \Phi'(\xi,\eta) \overline{b_{j_2}}(\xi) b_{j_1}(\eta) \, d \omega (\xi ) \, d \omega (\eta ) \, \frac{d t}{2 \pi}.\] Using the orbit map at \(\xi(t) \in \mathcal{O}(-t^2)\), we can view \(\Phi'\) as a function on \((G/H)^2\). Using the absolute convergence of the integral defining \(\Phi'\) and the smoothness of the vectors \(\Psi_1, \Psi_2\), we see that the function \(\Phi'\) is smooth (see (Nelson and Venkatesh 2021, sec. 18) for related arguments). In particular, \(\Phi'\) is Lipschitz near the origin, where it takes the value \(\Phi'(\xi(t),\xi(t)) = \int_H \Phi\). On the other hand, the factor \(\overline{b_{j_2}}(\xi) b_{j_1}(\eta)\) vanishes unless \(\xi,\eta = \xi(t) + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta)\); in that case, it is bounded in magnitude by \(\operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-4\delta + (1 - 2 \delta)(j_1 + j_2)})\), and we have \(\Phi'(\xi,\eta) = \int_H \Phi + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta)\). The volume of the set of such pairs \((\xi,\eta)\) is \(\operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{4 \delta})\). It follows that \[I_{j_1,j_2} \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{(1 - 2 \delta) (j_1 + j_2)}.\] In particular, since \(\delta\) is sufficiently small, we have \(I_{j_1,j_2} \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\delta\) if \((j_1,j_2) \neq (0,0)\). On the other hand, since \(b_0 = a^2\) and \(\int_{\mathcal{O}(-t^2)} a^2 = k(-t^2)\), we have \[I_{0,0} = \left(\int_H \Phi\right) \int_{t > 0} k(-t^2)^2 \, \frac{d t}{2 \pi} + \operatorname{O}({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\delta}).\] This completes the proof of the required estimate for \(\mathcal{I}(f)\).

Remark 8. An alternative proof may be obtained by first decomposing \(\langle g \cdot f, f \rangle\) over the spectrum of \(L^2(G)\) as the integral of the Hilbert–Schmidt inner products \(\langle \pi(g) \pi(f) \pi(g)^{-1}, \pi(f) \rangle\). This decomposition is reflected above, in \((8.7)\), at the level of coadjoint orbits.

§8.3. Error estimates

We now prepare for the verification of \((1.21)\), which requires some Lie-theoretic preliminaries. We begin with some trivial remarks concerning the complex plane which we hope convey a useful reference picture for the estimates to follow. There are two common choices of coordinates: the rectangular coordinates, described by real and imaginary part, and the polar coordinates, described by radius and angle. The former is adapted to addition, the latter to multiplication. If we restrict the radius to a fixed compact subset of the positive reals and the angle to a sufficiently small neighborhood of the origin, then the real part is likewise restricted to a fixed compact subset of the positive reals; moreover, the imaginary part and the angle are bounded from above and below by constant multiples of one another. These restrictions define a region in the complex plane. Given a scalar-valued function \(\phi\) on that region and a small scaling parameter \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}> 0\), there are two natural ways to rescale \(\phi\) so that its support concentrates along the positive reals: in rectangular coordinates (by scaling the imaginary part) or in polar coordinates (by scaling the angle). The two classes of rescaled functions obtained in this way resemble one another. We aim now to record some analogues and elaborations of these observations with the complex numbers replaced by the \(2 \times 2\) matrix algebra \(M\). Such considerations are natural because we are ultimately studying a problem in multiplicative harmonic analysis (the variance sums \(\mathcal{V}(f)\) attached to test functions \(f\) on the group \(G = \operatorname{PGL}_2(\mathbb{R})\)) using additive harmonic analysis (via theta functions attached to Schwartz functions \(\heartsuit^{\tau} f\) on the matrix algebra \(M = M_2(\mathbb{R})\)).

We denote by \(\mathfrak{g} = \mathfrak{s}\mathfrak{l}_2(\mathbb{R})\) the Lie algebra of \(G\). We may identify \(\mathfrak{g}\) with the subspace of traceless elements in \(M\); then \(M = \mathbb{R} \oplus \mathfrak{g}\), where \(\mathbb{R}\) is the subspace of scalar matrices.

Let \(\mathcal{R} \subseteq \mathbb{R}^\times_+\) and \(\mathcal{G} \subseteq \mathfrak{g}\) be precompact open subsets. We assume that \(0 \in \mathcal{G}\) and that \(\mathcal{G}\) is star-shaped: \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\mathcal{G} \subseteq \mathcal{G}\) for \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\in [0,1]\). We assume that \(\mathcal{G}\) is taken small enough in terms of \(\mathcal{R}\); in particular, we assume that the map \[\mathcal{R} \times \mathcal{G} \ni (r,x) \mapsto r^{1/2} \exp(x) \in {\mathop{\mathrm{GL}}}_2^+(\mathbb{R})\] is an analytic isomorphism onto its image, which we denote by \(\mathcal{M}\). We write \[(\rho,\theta) : \mathcal{M} \rightarrow \mathcal{R} \times \mathcal{G}\] for the inverse isomorphism. Thus for \(v \in \mathcal{M}\), we have \(\rho(v) = \sqrt{\det(v)}\), while \(\theta(v)\) is the logarithm of the image of \(v\) in \(G\). We informally regard \(\rho(v)\) and \(\theta(v)\) as the respective radial and angular parts of \(v\).

Every element of \(M_2(\mathbb{R})\) may be written uniquely in the form \(t + u\), where \(t \in \mathbb{R}\) and \(u \in \mathfrak{g}\). Write \(\gamma(t,u) = (\rho(t,u), \theta(t,u)) =: (r,x)\), say. Then \[r = \sqrt{t^2 - u^2}, \quad x = \frac{1}{2} \log \frac{t + u }{t - u}.\] We informally regard \((t,u)\) and \((r,x)\) as the respective rectangular and polar coordinates on \(\mathcal{M}\).

Since \(\mathcal{G}\) is small, we have \(t \neq 0\). Thus \(r,t\) are both constrained to lie in compact subsets of \(\mathbb{R}^\times\), and so \[\label{eq:r-t-same-size}\tag{8.8} |r| \asymp 1 \asymp |t|.\]

Moreover, \[\label{eq:x-u-same-size}\tag{8.9} |x| \asymp |u|.\] Indeed, we may expand the analytic map \[\label{eq:gamma-2-of-t-u}\tag{8.10} \mathbb{R} \oplus \mathfrak{g} \ni (t,u) \mapsto \theta(t+u) \in \mathcal{G}\] as a Taylor series \(\sum_{n \geqslant 1} c_n (u/t)^n\), and similarly for the inverse map. By \((8.8)\), it follows that \(x\) and \(u\) tend to zero simultaneously and at the same rate. Since the magnitudes of both are bounded from above, we deduce \((8.9)\).

We now fix a cutoff \(q \in C_c^\infty(\mathcal{M})\) and define a family of maps of Schwartz spaces \[\mathcal{S}(\mathfrak{g}) \rightarrow \mathcal{S}(M), \quad \phi \mapsto \Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}},\] indexed by \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\in (0,1)\), by the formula \[\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}(t+ u) := q(t + \mathop{\mathrm{h}}u) \phi({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-1} \theta(t + \mathop{\mathrm{h}}u)).\] Informally, \(\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}\) is obtained from \(\Phi_{1}\) by rescaling in two stages: first shrinking the angular support in polar coordinates, then stretching the imaginary support in rectangular coordinates. The reference picture discussed above hopefully renders the following observation unsurprising:

Lemma 23. The family of maps \(\phi \mapsto \Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}\) is equicontinuous for the Schwartz topology.

Proof. The derivatives of the map \((t,u) \mapsto q(t+\mathop{\mathrm{h}}u)\) are uniformly bounded.

Moreover, the derivatives of the map \((t,u) \mapsto {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-1} \theta(t + \mathop{\mathrm{h}}u)\) are bounded, uniformly for \(t+\mathop{\mathrm{h}}u \in \mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(q)\). Indeed, we may write each such derivative as a convergent Taylor series; applying the triangle inequality gives a finite bound for the magnitude of this series, and the observation following \((8.10)\) implies that this bound improves as \(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\) decreases.

By the chain rule, we deduce that the derivatives of \(\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}\) at \(t + u\) are dominated by derivatives of \(\phi\) at those elements \({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-1} \theta(t +\mathop{\mathrm{h}}u)\) for which \(t + \mathop{\mathrm{h}}u \in \mathop{\mathrm{supp}}(q)\). For such elements, the estimates \((8.8)\) and \((8.9)\) give \(|t| \asymp 1\) and \(|{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-1} \theta(t + \mathop{\mathrm{h}}u)| \asymp |u|\). Thus the rapid decay of the derivatives of \(\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}\) follows from that of \(\phi\). ◻

We now apply these considerations to establish \((1.21)\), which we copy here for convenience: \[\mathcal{E}_{\tau_1,\tau_2}( \heartsuit^{\tau_1} f, \heartsuit^{\tau_2} f) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1-\delta'}. \tag{\ref{eqn:req-E}}\] Let \(\tau \in \{\tau_1, \tau_2\}\). We assume \(\mathcal{R}\) taken large enough to contain the support of \(r \mapsto W(\tau r)\), and take \(\mathcal{G}\) small enough. We may assume that \(\mathop{\mathrm{Op}}\) was defined with respect to a cutoff supported in \(\mathcal{G}\). Set \[q^\tau(v) := \frac{W(\tau \rho(v)^2) }{ |\tau \rho(v)^2| } {\mathop{\mathrm{jac}}}^{-1/2} \chi'(\theta(v))\] and \(\phi := b^\vee\), with \(b\) as in §8.1. We see then by unwinding the definitions that for \(v \in \mathcal{M}\), \[\heartsuit^{\tau} f(v) = q^\tau(v) {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-3/2} \phi({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-1} \theta(v)).\] Let \(\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\tau\) be defined as in §8.3 using \(q^\tau\) and \(\phi\). Then \(\heartsuit^{\tau}f(t + \mathop{\mathrm{h}}u) = {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-3/2} \Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\tau(t + u)\). We may express this identity in terms of the normalized dilation operators \(D_y\) (see §1.8) as \[\heartsuit^{\tau} f = D_{1/\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} \Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^\tau,\] so by Theorem 3, \[\mathcal{E}_{\tau_1,\tau_2}(\heartsuit^{\tau_1} f, \heartsuit^{\tau_2} f) = \mathcal{E}_{\tau_1,\tau_2} (D_{1/\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} \Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\tau_1}, D_{1/\mathop{\mathrm{h}}} \Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\tau_2}) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{1} \log ({\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{-1}) \mathcal{C}(\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\tau_1}) \mathcal{C}(\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\tau_2}),\] for some fixed continuous seminorm \(\mathcal{C}\). We appeal now to the (\(\mathop{\mathrm{h}}\)-uniform) continuity of \(\phi \mapsto \Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\tau}\) noted in §8.3, together with the continuity of the map \(a \mapsto b\) composed with the Fourier transform \(b \mapsto b^\vee = \phi\), to write \(\mathcal{C}(\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\tau_1}) \mathcal{C}(\Phi_{\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{\tau_2}) = \operatorname{O}(\mathcal{C}(a)^2)\) for some fixed continuous seminorm \(\mathcal{C}\) on \(\mathcal{S}(\mathfrak{g}^\wedge)\). The definition of \(S^{-\infty}_{\delta}\) implies that \(\mathcal{C}(a) \ll {\mathop{\mathrm{h}}}^{- N \delta}\) for some fixed \(N \in \mathbb{Z}_{\geqslant 0}\), so we may conclude by taking \(\delta' := (2 N + 1) \delta\).

Remark 9. The proof of \((1.21)\) recorded above is a bit different from that of the corresponding estimate in (Nelson 2017), whose analogue here would be to exploit the smoothness of \(\Psi\) and the diagonal \(G\)-invariance of \((f,\Psi) \mapsto \mu(\pi(f),\Psi)\) (i.e., the “\(\mathop{\mathrm{SO}}(B_S^0)\)-invariance” noted in Theorem 4) to “fatten up” the symbol \(a\) under the adjoint action. The argument given here produces weaker estimates, but is a bit shorter and simpler.

The proof of Theorem 1 is now complete.

§8.4. The holomorphic analogue

Central to our analysis of microlocal lifts of Maass forms was the definition \((6.1)\) of \(\xi(t) \in \mathcal{O}(-t^2)\). We may reformulate our definition of \(H\) and choice of \(w\) as follows:

  • \(H\) is the centralizer of \(\xi(t)\) (for \(t \neq 0\)).

  • \(w\) is an element of \(N(H) - H\). Then \(\xi(t) = - w \cdot \xi(t)\).
